	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Faith</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about religious belief. For trust in people or other things, see <a href="/wiki/Trust_(emotion)" class="mw-redirect" title="Trust (emotion)">Trust (emotion)</a>. For other types of faith, see <a href="/wiki/Faith_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Faith (disambiguation)">Faith (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">confidence or trust in a person, thing, or concept</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;"><a href="/wiki/File:National_gallery_in_washington_d.c.,_mino_da_fiesole,_fede,_1475-1480.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/National_gallery_in_washington_d.c.%2C_mino_da_fiesole%2C_fede%2C_1475-1480.JPG/140px-National_gallery_in_washington_d.c.%2C_mino_da_fiesole%2C_fede%2C_1475-1480.JPG" decoding="async" width="140" height="356" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/National_gallery_in_washington_d.c.%2C_mino_da_fiesole%2C_fede%2C_1475-1480.JPG/210px-National_gallery_in_washington_d.c.%2C_mino_da_fiesole%2C_fede%2C_1475-1480.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/National_gallery_in_washington_d.c.%2C_mino_da_fiesole%2C_fede%2C_1475-1480.JPG/280px-National_gallery_in_washington_d.c.%2C_mino_da_fiesole%2C_fede%2C_1475-1480.JPG 2x" data-file-width="630" data-file-height="1600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:National_gallery_in_washington_d.c.,_mino_da_fiesole,_fede,_1475-1480.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Faith (Armani)</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Mino_da_Fiesole" title="Mino da Fiesole">Mino da Fiesole</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><b>Faith</b>, derived from Latin <i>fides</i> and Old French <i>feid</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-Oxford_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> is confidence or trust in a person, thing, or <span class="nowrap">concept.<sup id="cite_ref-Oxford_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></span> In the context of <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a>, one can define faith as <a href="/wiki/Confidence" title="Confidence">confidence</a> or <a href="/wiki/Trust_(social_sciences)" class="mw-redirect" title="Trust (social sciences)">trust</a> in a particular system of <a href="/wiki/Religious_belief" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious belief">religious belief</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
Religious people often think of faith as confidence based on a perceived degree of <a href="/wiki/Warrant_(philosophy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Warrant (philosophy)">warrant</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
while others who are more skeptical of religion tend to think of faith as simply belief without evidence.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Faith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The English word <i>faith</i> is thought to date from 1200–1250, from the Middle English <i>feith</i>, via Anglo-French <i>fed</i>, Old French <i>feid</i>, <i>feit</i> from Latin <i>fidem</i>, accusative of <i>fidēs</i> (trust), akin to <i>fīdere</i> (to trust).<sup id="cite_ref-dict_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dict-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Stages_of_faith_development">Stages of faith development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Faith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Stages of faith development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/James_W._Fowler#Stages_of_Faith" title="James W. Fowler">James W. Fowler §&#160;Stages of Faith</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/James_W._Fowler" title="James W. Fowler">James W. Fowler</a> (1940–2015) proposes a series of stages of faith-development (or <a href="/wiki/Spiritual_development" title="Spiritual development">spiritual development</a>) across the human life-span. His stages relate closely to the work of <a href="/wiki/Jean_Piaget" title="Jean Piaget">Piaget</a>, Erikson, and <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Kohlberg" title="Lawrence Kohlberg">Kohlberg</a> regarding aspects of psychological development in children and adults. Fowler defines faith as an activity of trusting, committing, and relating to the world based on a set of assumptions of how one is related to others and the world.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stages_of_faith">Stages of faith</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Faith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Stages of faith">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ol><li>Intuitive-Projective: a stage of confusion and of high impressionability through stories and <a href="/wiki/Ritual" title="Ritual">rituals</a> (pre-school period).</li>
<li>Mythic-Literal: a stage where provided information is accepted in order to conform with <a href="/wiki/Social_norm" title="Social norm">social norms</a> (school-going period).</li>
<li>Synthetic-Conventional: in this stage the faith acquired is concreted in the <a href="/wiki/Belief_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Belief system">belief system</a> with the forgoing of personification and replacement with <a href="/wiki/Authority" title="Authority">authority</a> in individuals or groups that represent one's beliefs (early-late adolescence).</li>
<li>Individuative-Reflective: in this stage the individual critically analyzes adopted and accepted faith with existing systems of faith. Disillusion or strengthening of faith happens in this stage. Based on needs, experiences and paradoxes (early adulthood).</li>
<li>Conjunctive faith: in this stage people realize the limits of <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a> and, facing the paradoxes or <a href="/wiki/Transcendence_(philosophy)" title="Transcendence (philosophy)">transcendence</a> of <a href="/wiki/Personal_life" title="Personal life">life</a>, accept the "mystery of life" and often return to the sacred stories and symbols of the pre-acquired or re-adopted faith system. This stage is called negotiated settling in life (mid-life).</li>
<li>Universalizing faith: this is the "enlightenment" stage where the individual comes out of all the existing systems of faith and lives life with universal principles of compassion and love and in service to others for upliftment, without worries and <a href="/wiki/Doubt" title="Doubt">doubt</a> (middle-late adulthood (45–65 years old and plus).<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A complete citation is needed (May 2018)">full citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></li></ol>
<p>No hard-and-fast rule requires individuals pursuing faith to go through all six stages. There is a high probability for individuals to be content and fixed in a particular stage for a lifetime; stages from 2-5 are such stages. Stage 6 is the summit of faith development. This state is often<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="This term requires quantification. (December 2015)">quantify</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> considered as "not fully" attainable.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_views">Religious views</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Faith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Religious views">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="Bah.C3.A1.27.C3.AD_Faith"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Bahá'í_Faith">Bahá'í Faith</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Faith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Bahá&#039;í Faith">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Role_of_faith_in_the_Baha%27i_Faith" class="mw-redirect" title="Role of faith in the Baha&#39;i Faith">Role of faith in the Baha'i Faith</a></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27%C3%AD_Faith" title="Bahá&#39;í Faith">Bahá'í Faith</a>, faith is meant, first, conscious knowledge, and second, the practice of good deeds,<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> ultimately the acceptance of the divine authority of the <a href="/wiki/Manifestations_of_God" class="mw-redirect" title="Manifestations of God">Manifestations of God</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> In the religion's view, faith and knowledge are both required for spiritual growth.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Faith involves more than outward obedience to this authority, but also must be based on a deep personal understanding of religious teachings.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Buddhism">Buddhism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Faith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Buddhism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Faith_in_Buddhism" title="Faith in Buddhism">Faith in Buddhism</a></div>
<p>Faith in Buddhism (<i><a href="/wiki/Pali_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Pali language">Pali</a>: <span lang="pi">saddhā</span></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Sanskrit_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Sanskrit language">Sanskrit</a>: <span lang="sa">śraddhā</span></i>) refers to a serene commitment in the practice of the Buddha's teaching and trust in enlightened or highly developed beings, such as <a href="/wiki/Buddha_(title)" title="Buddha (title)">Buddhas</a> or <i><a href="/wiki/Bodhisattvas" class="mw-redirect" title="Bodhisattvas">bodhisattvas</a></i> (those aiming to become a Buddha).<sup id="cite_ref-Gomez_2004_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gomez_2004-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJayatilleke1963388–9_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJayatilleke1963388–9-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Buddhists usually recognize multiple objects of faith, but many are especially devoted to one particular object of faith, such as one particular Buddha.<sup id="cite_ref-Gomez_2004_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gomez_2004-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJayatilleke1963386,_396–7_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJayatilleke1963386,_396–7-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Early_Buddhism" title="Early Buddhism">early Buddhism</a>, faith was focused on the <a href="/wiki/Triple_Gem" class="mw-redirect" title="Triple Gem">Triple Gem</a>, that is, <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" title="Gautama Buddha">Gautama Buddha</a>, his teaching (the <a href="/wiki/Dharma_(Buddhism)" class="mw-redirect" title="Dharma (Buddhism)">Dhamma</a>), and the community of spiritually developed followers, or the monastic community seeking enlightenment (the <a href="/wiki/Sangha" title="Sangha">Sangha</a>). Although offerings to the monastic community were valued highest, early Buddhism did not morally condemn peaceful offerings to <a href="/wiki/Deva_(Buddhism)" title="Deva (Buddhism)">deities</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamotte198874–5,_81_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamotte198874–5,_81-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> A faithful devotee was called <a href="/wiki/Upasaka_and_Upasika" class="mw-redirect" title="Upasaka and Upasika"><i>upāsaka</i> or <i>upāsika</i></a>, for which no formal declaration was required.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> In early Buddhism, personal verification was valued highest in attaining the truth, and sacred scriptures, reason or faith in a teacher were considered less valuable sources of authority.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> As important as faith was, it was a mere initial step to the path to <a href="/wiki/Pa%C3%B1%C3%B1%C4%81" class="mw-redirect" title="Paññā">wisdom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Enlightenment_(Buddhism)" class="mw-redirect" title="Enlightenment (Buddhism)">enlightenment</a>, and was obsolete or redefined at the final stage of that path.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamotte198849–50_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamotte198849–50-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJayatilleke1963384,_396–7_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJayatilleke1963384,_396–7-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While <a href="/wiki/Faith_in_Buddhism" title="Faith in Buddhism">faith in Buddhism</a> does not imply "blind faith", Buddhist practice nevertheless requires a degree of trust, primarily in the spiritual attainment of <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" title="Gautama Buddha">Gautama Buddha</a>. Faith in <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> centers on the understanding that the Buddha is an Awakened being, on his superior role as teacher, in the truth of his <a href="/wiki/Dharma" title="Dharma">Dharma</a> (spiritual teachings), and in his <a href="/wiki/Sangha_(Buddhism)" class="mw-redirect" title="Sangha (Buddhism)">Sangha</a> (community of spiritually developed followers). Faith in Buddhism can be summarised as faith in the <a href="/wiki/Three_Jewels" class="mw-redirect" title="Three Jewels">Three Jewels</a>: the Buddha, Dharma and Sangha. It is intended to lead to the goal of enlightenment, or <a href="/wiki/Bodhi" class="mw-redirect" title="Bodhi">bodhi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nirvana" title="Nirvana">Nirvana</a>. Volitionally, faith implies a resolute and courageous act of will. It combines the steadfast resolution that one will do a thing with the self-confidence that one can do it.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the later stratum of Buddhist history, especially <a href="/wiki/Mah%C4%81y%C4%81na_Buddhism" class="mw-redirect" title="Mahāyāna Buddhism">Mahāyāna Buddhism</a>, faith was given a much more important role.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarvey2013172_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarvey2013172-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The concept of the <a href="/wiki/Buddha_Nature" class="mw-redirect" title="Buddha Nature">Buddha Nature</a> was developed, as devotion to Buddhas and <i>bodhisattvas</i> residing in <a href="/wiki/Pure_Land" class="mw-redirect" title="Pure Land">Pure Lands</a> became commonplace.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> With the arising of the cult of the <a href="/wiki/Lotus_S%C5%ABtra" class="mw-redirect" title="Lotus Sūtra">Lotus Sūtra</a>, faith gained a central role in Buddhist practice,<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> which was further amplified with the development of devotion to the <a href="/wiki/Amitabha_Buddha" class="mw-redirect" title="Amitabha Buddha">Amitabha Buddha</a> in <a href="/wiki/Pure_Land_Buddhism" title="Pure Land Buddhism">Pure Land Buddhism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen2013123_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen2013123-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> In the Japanese form of Pure Land Buddhism, under the teachers <a href="/wiki/H%C5%8Dnen" title="Hōnen">Hōnen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shinran" title="Shinran">Shinran</a>, only <a href="/wiki/Shinjin" title="Shinjin">entrusting faith</a> toward the Amitabha Buddha was believed to be a fruitful form of practice, as the practice of celibacy, morality and other Buddhist disciplines were dismissed as no longer effective in this day and age, or contradicting the virtue of faith.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen2013122–3_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen2013122–3-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarvey2013230,_255_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarvey2013230,_255-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Faith was defined as a state similar to enlightenment, with a sense of self-negation and humility.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thus, the role of faith increased throughout Buddhist history. However, from the nineteenth century onward, <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_modernism" title="Buddhist modernism">Buddhist modernism</a> in countries like Sri Lanka and Japan, and also in the West, has downplayed and criticized the role of faith in Buddhism. Faith in Buddhism still has a role in modern Asia or the West, but is understood and defined differently from traditional interpretations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarvey2013378,_429,_444_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarvey2013378,_429,_444-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Within the <a href="/wiki/Dalit_Buddhist_Movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Dalit Buddhist Movement">Dalit Buddhist Movement</a> communities, taking <a href="/wiki/Three_Refuges" class="mw-redirect" title="Three Refuges">refuge</a> is defined not only as a religious, but also a political choice.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Christianity">Christianity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Faith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Christianity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Triumph_of_Faith_over_Idolatry_Theodon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Triumph_of_Faith_over_Idolatry_Theodon.jpg/180px-Triumph_of_Faith_over_Idolatry_Theodon.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="211" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Triumph_of_Faith_over_Idolatry_Theodon.jpg/270px-Triumph_of_Faith_over_Idolatry_Theodon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Triumph_of_Faith_over_Idolatry_Theodon.jpg/360px-Triumph_of_Faith_over_Idolatry_Theodon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1750" data-file-height="2050" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Triumph_of_Faith_over_Idolatry_Theodon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Triumph of Faith over <a href="/wiki/Idolatry" title="Idolatry">Idolatry</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Th%C3%A9odon" title="Jean-Baptiste Théodon">Jean-Baptiste Théodon</a> (1646–1713)</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Faith_in_Christianity" title="Faith in Christianity">Faith in Christianity</a></div>
<p>The word translated as "faith" in English-language editions of the New Testament, the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament_Greek" class="mw-redirect" title="New Testament Greek"> Greek</a> word <i>πίστις</i> (<i>pístis</i>), can also be translated as "belief", "faithfulness", or "trust".<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Christianity encompasses various views regarding the nature of faith. Some see faith as being persuaded or convinced that something is true.<sup id="cite_ref-Wilkin2012_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wilkin2012-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> In this view, a person believes something when they are presented with adequate evidence that it is true. The theologian Saint <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a> did not hold that faith is mere opinion: on the contrary, he held that it represents a mean (understood in the Platonic sense) between excessive reliance on science (i.e. demonstration) and excessive reliance on opinion.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Numerous views discuss the results of faith. Some believe that true faith results in good works, while others believe that while faith in <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a> brings eternal life, it does not necessarily result in good works.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Regardless of which approach to faith a Christian takes, all agree that the Christian faith is aligned with the ideals and the example of the life of <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a>. The Christian sees the mystery of <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a> and his <a href="/wiki/Divine_grace" title="Divine grace"> grace</a>, and seeks to know and become obedient to God. To a Christian, faith is not static but causes one to learn more of God and to grow; Christian faith has its origin in God.<sup id="cite_ref-Wuerl2004_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wuerl2004-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The definition of faith given by the author of the Epistle to the Hebrews at Hebrews 11:1 carries particular weight with Christians who respect the Bible as the source of divine truth. There the author writes:
</p><p>"Now faith is the substance of things hoped for, the evidence of things not seen." — King James Version
</p><p>"Now faith is the assurance that what we hope for will come about and the certainty that what we cannot see exists." — International Standard Version
</p><p>“The naive or inexperienced person[is easily misled and believes every word he hears, but the prudent man is discreet and astute.” (Proverbs 14:15, Amplified Bible) The Christian apostle Paul wrote: "Test everything that is said to be sure it is true, and if it is, then accept it." (1 Thessalonians 5:21, Living Bible)
</p><p>In Christianity, faith causes change as it seeks a greater understanding of God. Faith is not only <a href="/wiki/Fideism" title="Fideism">fideism</a> or simple obedience to a set of rules or statements.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Before Christians have faith, they must understand in whom and in what they have faith. Without understanding, there cannot be true faith, and that understanding is built on the foundation of the community of believers, the scriptures and traditions and on the personal experiences of the <a href="/wiki/Belief" title="Belief"> believer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> In English translations of the New Testament, the word "faith" generally corresponds to the Greek noun πίστις (<i>pistis</i>) or to the Greek verb πιστεύω (<i>pisteuo</i>), meaning "to trust, to have confidence, faithfulness, to be reliable, to assure".<sup id="cite_ref-Thomas1981_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas1981-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Christians may recognise different <b>degrees of faith</b> when they encourage each other to and themselves strive to develop, grow, and/or deepen their faith.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
This may imply that one can measure faith. Willingness to undergo <a href="/wiki/Martyrdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Martyrdom">martyrdom</a> indicates a proxy for depth of faith, but does not provide an everyday measurement for the average contemporary Christian. Within the <a href="/wiki/Calvinist" class="mw-redirect" title="Calvinist">Calvinist</a> tradition the degree of prosperity<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
may serve as an analog of level of faith.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
Other Christian strands may rely on personal self-evaluation to measure the intensity of an individual's faith, with associated difficulties in calibrating to any scale. Solemn affirmations of a <a href="/wiki/Creed" title="Creed">creed</a> (a statement of faith) provide  broad measurements of details. Various tribunals of the <a href="/wiki/Inquisition" title="Inquisition">Inquisition</a>, however, concerned themselves with precisely evaluating the orthodoxy of the faith of those it examined - in order to acquit or to punish in varying degrees.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Christian_apologetic_views">Christian apologetic views</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Faith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Christian apologetic views">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In contrast to <a href="/wiki/Richard_Dawkins" title="Richard Dawkins">Richard Dawkins</a>' view of faith as "blind trust, in the absence of evidence, even in the teeth of evidence",<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alister_McGrath" title="Alister McGrath">Alister McGrath</a> quotes the Oxford Anglican theologian W. H. Griffith-Thomas (1861–1924), who states that faith is "not blind, but intelligent" and that it "commences with the conviction of the mind based on adequate evidence...", which McGrath sees as "a good and reliable definition, synthesizing the core elements of the characteristic Christian understanding of faith".<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>American biblical scholar <a href="/wiki/Archibald_Thomas_Robertson" title="Archibald Thomas Robertson">Archibald Thomas Robertson</a> stated that the Greek word <i>pistis</i> used for faith in the New Testament (over two hundred forty times), and rendered "assurance" in Acts 17:31 (KJV), is "an old verb meaning "to furnish", used regularly by Demosthenes for bringing forward evidence."<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Tom Price (Oxford Centre for Christian Apologetics) affirms that when the New Testament talks about faith positively it only uses words derived from the Greek root [pistis] which means "to be persuaded".<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>British Christian apologist <a href="/wiki/John_Lennox" title="John Lennox">John Lennox</a> argues that "faith conceived as belief that lacks warrant is very different from faith conceived as belief that has warrant". He states that "the use of the adjective 'blind' to describe 'faith' indicates that faith is not necessarily, or always, or indeed normally, blind". "The validity, or warrant, of faith or belief depends on the strength of the evidence on which the belief is based." "We all know how to distinguish between blind faith and evidence-based faith. We are well aware that faith is only justified if there is evidence to back it up." "Evidence-based faith is the normal concept on which we base our everyday lives."<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Peter S Williams<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> holds that "the classic Christian tradition has always valued rationality, and does not hold that faith involves the complete abandonment of reason while believing in the teeth of evidence."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (February 2015)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Quoting Moreland, faith is defined as "a trust in and commitment to what we have reason to believe is true."
</p><p>Regarding <a href="/wiki/Doubting_Thomas" title="Doubting Thomas">doubting Thomas</a> in John 20:24-31, Williams points out that "Thomas wasn't asked to believe without evidence". He was asked to believe on the basis of the other disciples' testimony. Thomas initially lacked the first-hand experience of the evidence that had convinced them... Moreover, the reason John gives for recounting these events is that what he saw is evidence... Jesus did many other miraculous signs in the presence of his disciples...But these are written that you may believe that Jesus is the Christ, the son of God, and that believing ye might have life in his name. John 20:30,31.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Concerning <a href="/wiki/Doubting_Thomas" title="Doubting Thomas">doubting Thomas</a>, Michael R. Allen wrote, "Thomas's definition of faith implies adherence to conceptual propositions for the sake of personal knowledge, knowledge of and about a person <i>qua</i> person".<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Kenneth Boa and Robert M. Bowman Jr. describe a classic understanding of faith that is referred to<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (February 2015)">by whom?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>as <i><a href="/wiki/Evidentialism" title="Evidentialism">evidentialism</a></i>, and which is part of a larger epistemological tradition called <i>classical <a href="/wiki/Foundationalism" title="Foundationalism">foundationalism</a></i>, which is accompanied by <i><a href="/wiki/Deontologism" class="mw-redirect" title="Deontologism">deontologism</a></i>, which holds that humans have an obligation to regulate their beliefs in accordance with evidentialist structures.
</p><p>They show how this can go too far,<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Alvin_Plantinga" title="Alvin Plantinga">Alvin Plantinga</a> deals with it. While Plantinga upholds that faith may be the result of evidence testifying to the reliability of the source (of the truth claims), yet he sees having faith as being the result of hearing the truth of the gospel with the internal persuasion by the Holy Spirit moving and enabling him to believe. "Christian belief is produced in the believer by the internal instigation of the Holy Spirit, endorsing the teachings of Scripture, which is itself divinely inspired by the Holy Spirit. The result of the work of the Holy Spirit is faith."<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Catholicism">Catholicism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Faith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Catholicism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The four-part <i><a href="/wiki/Catechism_of_the_Catholic_Church" title="Catechism of the Catholic Church">Catechism of the Catholic Church</a></i> (CCC) gives Part One to "The Profession of Faith".  This section describes the content of faith.  It elaborates and expands particularly upon the <a href="/wiki/Apostles%27_Creed" title="Apostles&#39; Creed">Apostles' Creed</a>.  CCC 144 initiates a section on the "Obedience of Faith".
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a> of <a href="/wiki/Pope_John_Paul_II" title="Pope John Paul II">Pope John Paul II</a>, faith is understood in personal terms as a trusting commitment of person to person and thus involves Christian commitment to the divine person of <a href="/wiki/Jesus_Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Jesus Christ">Jesus Christ</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Church_of_Jesus_Christ_of_Latter-day_Saints">The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Faith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some alternative, yet impactful, ideas regarding the nature of faith were presented<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (September 2019)">by whom?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> in a collection of sermons now presented as <cite class="citation book"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://rsc.byu.edu/archived/lectures-faith-historical-perspective/lectures-faith-1990-edited-version/lecture-1"><i>Lectures on Faith</i></a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Lectures+on+Faith&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Frsc.byu.edu%2Farchived%2Flectures-faith-historical-perspective%2Flectures-faith-1990-edited-version%2Flecture-1&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFaith" .cs1-visible-error{font-size:100%}.mw-parser-output .cs1-maint{display:none;color:#33aa33;margin-left:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .cs1-subscription,.mw-parser-output .cs1-registration,.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-wl-right{padding-right:0.2em}</style>
</p>
<ol><li>Lecture 1 explains what faith is;</li>
<li>Lecture 2 describes how mankind comes to know about God;</li>
<li>Lectures 3 and 4 make clear the necessary and unchanging attributes of God;</li>
<li>Lecture 5 deals with the nature of God the Father, his Son Jesus Christ, and the Holy Ghost;</li>
<li>Lecture 6 proclaims that the willingness to sacrifice all earthly things is prerequisite to gaining faith unto salvation;</li>
<li>Lecture 7 treats the fruits of faith—perspective, power, and eventually perfection.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="Please supply a full or month-and-year">date&#160;missing</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></li></ol>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hinduism">Hinduism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Faith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Hinduism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ahimsa" title="Ahimsa">Ahimsa</a>, also referred to as <a href="/wiki/Nonviolence" title="Nonviolence">nonviolence</a>, is the fundamental tenet of Hinduism which advocates harmonious and peaceful co-existence and evolutionary growth in grace and wisdom for all humankind unconditionally.
</p><p>In Hinduism, most of the Vedic prayers begins with the chants of Om. Om is the Sanskrit symbol that amazingly resonates the peacefulness ensconced within one's higher self. Om is considered to have a profound effect on the body and mind of the one who chants and also creates a calmness, serenity, healing, strength of its own to prevail within and also in the surrounding environment.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islam">Islam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Faith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Islam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Iman_(concept)" title="Iman (concept)">Iman (concept)</a></div>
<p>In Islam, a believer's faith in the metaphysical aspects of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> is called <i>Iman</i> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl"><span style="font-size:large;">الإيمان</span></span>&#8206;), which is complete submission to the will of God, not unquestionable or blind belief.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> A man must build his faith on well-grounded convictions beyond any reasonable doubt and above uncertainty.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> According to the Quran, Iman must be accompanied by righteous deeds and the two together are necessary for entry into <a href="/wiki/Jannah" title="Jannah">Paradise</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> In the Hadith of Gabriel, <i>Iman</i> in addition to <i>Islam</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Ihsan" title="Ihsan">Ihsan</a></i> form the three dimensions of the Islamic religion.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> referred to the <a href="/wiki/Iman_(concept)#The_Six_Axioms_of_Faith" title="Iman (concept)">six axioms of faith</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Hadith_of_Gabriel" title="Hadith of Gabriel">Hadith of Gabriel</a>: "Iman is that you believe in God and His Angels and His Books and His Messengers and the Hereafter and the good and evil fate [ordained by your God]."<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> The first five are mentioned together in the Qur'an<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> The Quran states that faith can grow with remembrance of God.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> The Qur'an also states that nothing in this world should be dearer to a true believer than faith.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Judaism">Judaism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Faith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Judaism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Jewish_principles_of_faith" title="Jewish principles of faith">Jewish principles of faith</a></div>
<p>Judaism recognizes the positive value of <i>Emunah</i><sup id="cite_ref-chabad_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chabad-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> (generally translated as faith, trust in God) and the negative status of the <i><a href="/wiki/Epikoros" title="Epikoros">Apikorus</a></i> (heretic), but faith is not as stressed or as central as it is in other religions, especially compared with <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> It could be a necessary means for being a practicing religious Jew, but the emphasis is placed on true <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a>, true <a href="/wiki/Prophecy#Judaism" title="Prophecy">prophecy</a> and practice rather than on faith itself. Very rarely does it relate to any teaching that must be believed.<sup id="cite_ref-Brueggemann2002_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brueggemann2002-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Judaism does not require one to explicitly identify God (a key tenet of <a href="/wiki/Christian_faith" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian faith">Christian faith</a>, which is called <a href="/wiki/Avodah_Zarah" title="Avodah Zarah">Avodah Zarah</a> in Judaism, a minor form of <a href="/wiki/Idolatry" title="Idolatry">idol worship</a>, a big sin and strictly forbidden to Jews). Rather, in Judaism, one is to honour a (personal) idea of God, supported by the many principles quoted in the Talmud to define Judaism, mostly by what it is not. Thus there is no established formulation of <a href="/wiki/Jewish_principles_of_faith" title="Jewish principles of faith">Jewish principles of faith</a> which are mandatory for all (observant) <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a>.
</p><p>In the Jewish scriptures trust in God – <i>Emunah</i> – refers to how God acts toward his people and how they are to respond to him; it is rooted in the everlasting covenant established in the <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">Torah</a>, notably<sup id="cite_ref-Brueggemann2002_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brueggemann2002-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Deuteronomy 7:9:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Know, therefore, that the Lord, your God He is God, the faithful God, Who keeps the covenant and loving kindness with those who love Him and keep His commandments to a thousand generations.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup></p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite><a href="/wiki/Tanakh" class="mw-redirect" title="Tanakh">Tanakh</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0507.htm#9">Deuteronomy 7:9</a></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>The specific tenets that compose required belief and their application to the times have been disputed throughout Jewish history. Today many, but not all, <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Jew" class="mw-redirect" title="Orthodox Jew">Orthodox Jews</a> have accepted <a href="/wiki/Maimonides" title="Maimonides">Maimonides</a>' Thirteen Principles of Belief.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A traditional example of <i>Emunah</i> as seen in the Jewish annals is found in the person of <a href="/wiki/Abraham" title="Abraham">Abraham</a>. On a number of occasions, Abraham both accepts statements from God that seem impossible and offers obedient actions in response to direction from God to do things that seem implausible (see <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Genesis" title="Book of Genesis">Genesis</a> 12-15).
</p>
<blockquote><p>"The <a href="/wiki/Talmud" title="Talmud">Talmud</a> describes how a thief also believes in G‑d: On the brink of his forced entry, as he is about to risk his life—and the life of his victim—he cries out with all sincerity, 'G‑d help me!' The thief has faith that there is a G‑d who hears his cries, yet it escapes him that this G‑d may be able to provide for him without requiring that he abrogate G‑d’s will by stealing from others. For <i>emunah</i> to affect him in this way he needs study and contemplation."<sup id="cite_ref-chabad_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chabad-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sikhism">Sikhism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Faith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Sikhism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Sikhism" title="Sikhism">Sikhism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Five_Ks" class="mw-redirect" title="Five Ks">Five Ks</a></div>
<p>Faith itself is not a religious concept in Sikhism. However, the five Sikh symbols, known as Kakaars or <a href="/wiki/Five_Ks" class="mw-redirect" title="Five Ks">Five Ks</a> (in Punjabi known as pañj kakkē or pañj kakār), are sometimes referred to as the <i>Five articles of Faith</i>. The articles include <i><a href="/wiki/Kesh_(Sikhism)" title="Kesh (Sikhism)">kēs</a></i> (uncut hair), <i><a href="/wiki/Kanga_(Sikhism)" class="mw-redirect" title="Kanga (Sikhism)">kaṅghā</a></i> (small wooden comb), <i><a href="/wiki/Kara_(Sikhism)" title="Kara (Sikhism)">kaṛā</a></i> (circular steel or iron bracelet), <i><a href="/wiki/Kirpan" title="Kirpan">kirpān</a></i> (sword/dagger), and <i><a href="/wiki/Kaccha" class="mw-redirect" title="Kaccha">kacchera</a></i> (special undergarment). Baptised <a href="/wiki/Sikhs" title="Sikhs">Sikhs</a> are bound to wear those five articles of faith, at all times, to save them from bad company and keep them close to God.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Epistemological_validity">Epistemological validity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Faith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Epistemological validity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There is a wide spectrum of opinion with respect to the <a href="/wiki/Epistemological" class="mw-redirect" title="Epistemological">epistemological</a> validity of faith<sup id="cite_ref-isbn0-06-065292-6_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-isbn0-06-065292-6-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> - that is, whether it is a reliable way to acquire true beliefs.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fideism">Fideism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Faith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Fideism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fideism" title="Fideism">Fideism</a></div>
<p>Fideism is an <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemological theory</a> which maintains that faith is independent of <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">reason</a>, or that reason and faith are hostile to each other and faith is superior at arriving at particular <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">truths</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Natural_theology" title="Natural theology">natural theology</a>). Fideism is not a synonym for religious belief, but describes a particular philosophical proposition in regard to the relationship between faith's appropriate jurisdiction at arriving at truths, contrasted against reason. It states that faith is needed to determine some philosophical and religious truths, and it questions the ability of reason to arrive at all truth. The word and concept had its origin in the mid- to late-19th century by way of <a href="/wiki/Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic">Catholic</a> thought, in a movement called <a href="/wiki/Traditionalism_(Catholicism)" class="mw-redirect" title="Traditionalism (Catholicism)">Traditionalism</a>. The Roman Catholic <a href="/wiki/Magisterium" title="Magisterium">Magisterium</a> has, however, repeatedly condemned <a href="/wiki/Fideism" title="Fideism">fideism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Support">Support</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Faith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Support">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Religious_epistemology" title="Religious epistemology">Religious epistemologists</a> have formulated and defended reasons for the rationality of accepting belief in God without the support of an argument.<sup id="cite_ref-InternetReligious_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-InternetReligious-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Some religious epistemologists hold that belief in God is more analogous to belief in a person than belief in a scientific hypothesis. Human relations demand trust and commitment. If belief in God is more like belief in other persons, then the trust that is appropriate to persons will be appropriate to God. American <a href="/wiki/Psychologist" title="Psychologist">psychologist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philosopher" title="Philosopher">philosopher</a> <a href="/wiki/William_James" title="William James">William James</a> offers a similar argument in his lecture <i><a href="/wiki/The_Will_to_Believe" title="The Will to Believe">The Will to Believe</a>.</i><sup id="cite_ref-InternetReligious_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-InternetReligious-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WillToBelieve_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WillToBelieve-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Foundationalism" title="Foundationalism">Foundationalism</a> is a view about the structure of justification or <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-InternetFoundational_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-InternetFoundational-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-InternetFoundational_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-InternetFoundational-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Foundationalism holds that all knowledge and <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_justification" title="Theory of justification">justified belief</a> are ultimately based upon what are called <a href="/wiki/Basic_belief" title="Basic belief">properly basic beliefs</a>. This position is intended to resolve the <a href="/wiki/Regress_argument" title="Regress argument">infinite regress</a> problem in <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a>. According to foundationalism, a belief is epistemically justified only if it is justified by properly basic beliefs. One of the significant developments in foundationalism is the rise of <a href="/wiki/Reformed_epistemology" title="Reformed epistemology">reformed epistemology</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-InternetFoundational_82-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-InternetFoundational-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Reformed epistemology is a view about the epistemology of religious belief, which holds that belief in God can be properly basic. <a href="/wiki/Analytic_philosophy" title="Analytic philosophy">Analytic</a> philosophers <a href="/wiki/Alvin_Plantinga" title="Alvin Plantinga">Alvin Plantinga</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Wolterstorff" title="Nicholas Wolterstorff">Nicholas Wolterstorff</a> develop this view.<sup id="cite_ref-Plantinga1983_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Plantinga1983-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Plantinga holds that an individual may rationally believe in God even though the individual does not possess sufficient evidence to convince an agnostic. One difference between reformed epistemology and <a href="/wiki/Fideism" title="Fideism">fideism</a> is that the former requires defence against known objections, whereas the latter might dismiss such objections as irrelevant.<sup id="cite_ref-StanfordReligious_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordReligious-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Plantinga has developed reformed epistemology in <i>Warranted Christian Belief</i> as a form of <a href="/wiki/Externalism" title="Externalism">externalism</a> that holds that the <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_justification" title="Theory of justification">justification</a> conferring factors for a belief may include external factors.<sup id="cite_ref-Plantinga2000_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Plantinga2000-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Some <a href="/wiki/Theism" title="Theism">theistic</a> philosophers have defended theism by granting <a href="/wiki/Evidentialism" title="Evidentialism">evidentialism</a> but supporting theism through deductive arguments whose premises are considered justifiable. Some of these arguments are probabilistic, either in the sense of having weight but being inconclusive, or in the sense of having a <a href="/wiki/Probability" title="Probability">mathematical probability</a> assigned to them.<sup id="cite_ref-InternetReligious_80-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-InternetReligious-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Notable in this regard are the cumulative arguments presented by <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">British</a> philosopher <a href="/wiki/Basil_Mitchell_(academic)" title="Basil Mitchell (academic)">Basil Mitchell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Analytic_philosophy" title="Analytic philosophy">analytic</a> philosopher <a href="/wiki/Richard_Swinburne" title="Richard Swinburne">Richard Swinburne</a>, whose arguments are based on <a href="/wiki/Bayesian_probability" title="Bayesian probability">Bayesian probability</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mitchell1973_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchell1973-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Swinburne1979_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swinburne1979-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> In a notable exposition of his arguments, Swinburne appeals to an inference for the best explanation.<sup id="cite_ref-Forrest1996_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forrest1996-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Swinburne1997_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swinburne1997-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Professor_of_Mathematics" class="mw-redirect" title="Professor of Mathematics">Professor of Mathematics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philosopher_of_science" class="mw-redirect" title="Philosopher of science">philosopher of science</a> at <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">University of Oxford</a> <a href="/wiki/John_Lennox" title="John Lennox">John Lennox</a> has stated, "Faith is not a leap in the dark; it’s the exact opposite. It’s a commitment based on <a href="/wiki/Evidence" title="Evidence">evidence</a>… It is irrational to reduce all faith to blind faith and then subject it to ridicule. That provides a very anti-intellectual and convenient way of avoiding intelligent discussion.” He criticises <a href="/wiki/Richard_Dawkins" title="Richard Dawkins">Richard Dawkins</a> as a famous proponent of asserting that faith equates to holding a belief without evidence, thus that it is possible to hold belief without evidence, for failing to provide evidence for this assertion.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (May 2018)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Faith&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Russell" title="Bertrand Russell">Bertrand Russell</a> wrote:<sup id="cite_ref-auto_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Christians hold that their faith does good, but other faiths do harm. At any rate, they hold this about the communist faith. What I wish to maintain is that all faiths do harm. We may define “faith” as a firm belief in something for which there is no evidence. Where there is evidence, no one speaks of “faith.” We do not speak of faith that two and two are four or that the earth is round. We only speak of faith when we wish to substitute emotion for evidence. The substitution of emotion for evidence is apt to lead to strife, since different groups substitute different emotions. Christians have faith in the Resurrection; communists have faith in <a href="/wiki/Labor_theory_of_value" title="Labor theory of value">Marx’s Theory of Value</a>. Neither faith can be defended rationally, and each therefore is defended by propaganda and, if necessary, by war.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>Will Religious Faith Cure Our Troubles?</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_biology" title="Evolutionary biology">Evolutionary biologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Richard_Dawkins" title="Richard Dawkins">Richard Dawkins</a> criticizes all faith by generalizing from specific faith in propositions that conflict directly with scientific evidence.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> He describes faith as belief without evidence; a process of active non-thinking. He states that it is a practice that only degrades our understanding of the natural world by allowing anyone to make a claim about nature that is based solely on their personal thoughts, and possibly distorted perceptions, that does not require testing against nature, has no ability to make reliable and consistent predictions, and is not subject to peer review.<sup id="cite_ref-Is_Science_a_Religion?_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Is_Science_a_Religion?-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Philosophy professor <a href="/wiki/Peter_Boghossian" title="Peter Boghossian">Peter Boghossian</a> argues that reason and evidence are the only way to determine which "claims about the world are likely true". Different religious traditions make different religious claims, and Boghossian asserts that faith alone cannot resolve conflicts between these without evidence. He gives as an example of the belief held by that Muslims that <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> (who died in the year 632) was the last prophet, and the contradictory belief held by Mormons that <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Smith" title="Joseph Smith">Joseph Smith</a> (born in 1805) was a prophet. Boghossian asserts that faith has no "built-in corrective mechanism". For factual claims, he gives the example of the belief that the Earth is 4,000 years old. With only faith and no reason or evidence, he argues, there is no way to correct this claim if it is inaccurate. Boghossian advocates thinking of faith either as "belief without evidence" or "pretending to know things you don't know".<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Max_Weber" title="Max Weber">Weber, Max</a> (2002) [1905]. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=4MmligHndssC"><i>The Protestant Ethic and the 'Spirit' of Capitalism: and Other Writings</i></a>. New York: Penguin. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781101098479" title="Special:BookSources/9781101098479"><bdi>9781101098479</bdi></a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">25 September</span> 2019</span>. <q>In the course of its development, Calvinism made a positive addition: the idea of the necessity of <i>putting one's faith to the test</i> [<i>Bewährung des Glaubens</i>] in secular working life. [...] It thus provided the <i>positive</i> motivation [<i>Antrieb</i>] for asceticism, and with the firm establishment of its ethics in the doctrine of predestination, the spiritual aristocracy of the monks, who stood outside and above the world, was replaced by the spiritual aristocracy of the saints <i>in</i> the world, predestined by God from eternity [...].</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Protestant+Ethic+and+the+%27Spirit%27+of+Capitalism%3A+and+Other+Writings&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.series=Penguin+twentieth-century+classics&amp;rft.pub=Penguin&amp;rft.date=2002&amp;rft.isbn=9781101098479&amp;rft.aulast=Weber&amp;rft.aufirst=Max&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3D4MmligHndssC&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFaith" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-50"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-50">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
<cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Edward_Peters_(scholar)" title="Edward Peters (scholar)">Peters, Edward</a> (1989) [1988]. "The Inquisition in Literature and Art". <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=TnqLow3iKd4C"><i>Inquisition</i></a> (reprint ed.). Berkeley: University of California Press. p.&#160;225. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780520066304" title="Special:BookSources/9780520066304"><bdi>9780520066304</bdi></a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">25 September</span> 2019</span>. <q>The costuming of those convicted [...] was the result of careful planning and indicated specific gradations of guilt. There was never a single, simple <i><a href="/wiki/Sanbenito" title="Sanbenito">sanbenito</a></i>, for example, but a different kind of <i>sanbenito</i> for different crimes and degrees of heresy, with corresponding headgear [...]. The garb of the penitents, the procession with inquisitorial banners and crosses, the careful design of the seating and sequence of the ceremony made the <a href="/wiki/Auto-da-f%C3%A9" title="Auto-da-fé"> <i>auto-de-fé</i></a> itself 'a work of art [...]' [...]. [...] The aim of the <i>auto-de-fé</i>, as its name suggests, is the '<i>act</i> of faith,' that is, the liturgical demonstration of the truth of the faith and the error and evil of its enemies.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=The+Inquisition+in+Literature+and+Art&amp;rft.btitle=Inquisition&amp;rft.place=Berkeley&amp;rft.pages=225&amp;rft.edition=reprint&amp;rft.pub=University+of+California+Press&amp;rft.date=1989&amp;rft.isbn=9780520066304&amp;rft.aulast=Peters&amp;rft.aufirst=Edward&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DTnqLow3iKd4C&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFaith" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
